       The proposed Labor ReformandSocial DevelopmentPolicyLoan(LaRSDPL 111)
       would: (a) strengthenColombia's socialprotectionsystemandimprovethe
       delivery of social services, (b) raise humancapitalformation, (c) improve
       employability, and (d) enhance monitoringandevaluationsystems for better
       transparency, social oversight, andresults management inthe social sectors.

       These objectives wouldbe achieved by     (9 developing andimplementing an
       integratedandmore efficient socialprotectionsystemthrough additional
       regulations andprocessesto improve coverage andtargeting of socialprotection
       programs ineducation, health, earlychildhooddevelopment, labor andjob u-aining,
       (ii)implementing Law715, which inter alia, introduces incentives to enhance and
       measurequalityinthe education sector,     (qincreasing labor force opportunities by
       the implementation of Law789 andPolicyAgreement (CONPES) 081to create a
       NationalJob TrainingSystem, and (iv) buildqmonitoringandevaluationtools
       andprocessesto enablepublic oversight andimprovepolicymakmginthe social
       andlabor sectors.

                                                  TOCOLOMBIA



                                             I. INTRODUCTION

The Labor Reform and Social Development Policy LoanI11 (LaRSDPL111) is a one-tranche operation
buildingonthe achievements of the Programmatic Labor ReformandSocial Structural Adjustment Loan
Q?LaRSSAL), which closed on Maxh 2005.1 The proposed operation, as was the PLaRsSAL, is
designed to support the Government of Colombia's (Goq objectives to: (a) strengthen social protection;
(b) raise humancapital formation; (c) increase employability; and (d) enhance monitoringandevaluation
system for better transparency, social oversight, and results management inthe social sectors. These
objectives are closely algned with the Uribe Administration's National Development Plan (NDP) that
was formulated inearly 2003 to guide policythrough2006. This document proposes the LaRSDPLI11
for the amount of US$2OO million, in a s q l e tranche, to support the implementation of the final
initiatives in the NDP and to facilitate continuity in select social programs across presidential
administrations.*

The strategies outlined inthe NDP have beenlargely successful at getting the countryback ontrack after
the 1998/99 economic crisis. The effects of the crisis are fading as GDP growthresumed to 5.1 percent
annuallyin2005, private investment doubled to 11 percent between 1999 and2004, andunemployment
rates are at their lowest point since the crisis. Poverty rates continue their downward trend and the
coverage of social programs has expanded significantly without putting additional strain on the national
budget. The efforts to provide employment opportunities, expand the quality and quantityof education
services, provide affordable healthcare to even the poorest Colombians, expand the social safetynet, and
buildtools to allow for social oversight and transparency are keyfactors for poverty reduction and are
expected to reduce poverty inthe long-run. Security conditions have improved significantly since 2002
and the GoC has brokered several demobilization agreements with guenilla f&ters                    and illegal self-
defense forces3 after parsof previous failures, further improvingthe welfare of the population.

Despite these advances, significant challenges in the social sectors persist. Although poverty rates are
dechmg, short-run growth has not been sufficientlypro-poor, demonstrating svuctural constraints to
poverty reduction that require more than strong growth rates to resolve them.                     Generous public
expenditures intraining have not been adequate as the market has been slowto absorb labor, resulting in
the stubbornlyhighunemployment rates, relative to the pre-crisis period? While education coverage and
quality have been increasing at all levels, Colombia stiU lags behind the Latin America and Caribbean
Region (LAC$interms of coverage? and behind the world interms of quality of education. Manypoor

numbering to signal the continuity.

still do not have adequate protection against financial losses from illness nor access to good quality basic
health services although health insurance coverage has expanded since 2002 and health expenditures are
nearly double those (as a proportionof GDP) of the Latin American average.            The young from poor
families are at a particular riskdue to nearlya decade of limitedhealthandearlyeducation services. With
the re-election of President Uribe, the effort to address these concerns is likelyto continue.

The Bank's engagement with the Colombian social and labor sectors began with the Pastrana
Administration (1998-2002) and strengthened through the period of the Uribe Government (2002-2006).
The collaboration was initiated during the 1998/99 crisis via Bank support to emergency programs
Fu&
approved August 1,2000) was the Bank's firstwell
         enA&       and EnpSo enA&         - as                                                             -
                                                      as sector work A sectoral adjustment loan (SEW,
                                                 initiative inthe policyside of the social sectors, andserved
to bridge the Bank's dialogue on social and labor sectors from the Pastranato the Uribe Government.
Shortly after President Uribe took office, the Bank prepared a two-operation Programmatic Labor and
Social Structural Adjustment Loan (PLaRSSAL I,approved September 20038 and PLaRSSAL 11,
approved November 20049). The operations were closely akgned with the GoC's goals to support the
expansion of social services in a fiscally constrained environment by increasing efficiency in social and
labor programs and creating a more conducive policyenvironment. A Technical Assistance Loan(TAL             -
approved November 2004) was also prepared to support the implementation of the PLaRSSAL, with a
focus on regulating and implementing reforms in the labor, social expenditure, sub-national social
protection, social monitoring and evaluation (M&E),education, and health sectors. A rich analytical
program supported by the Bank provided the analytical underpinnings to the lending operations,
includingtechnical andfinancial support to analytical work bythe client inthe labor and social sectors.10
The client's appreciation for the extensive and cohesive Bank program is reflected in the Letter of
DevelopmentPolicy(Annex I).

The Colombia Country Assistance Strategy Progress Report (CASPRJ11 that was discussed by the
Executive Directors on September 29,2005, proposes a thirdloanto buildonthe foundations created by
the PLaRSSALs. Originally, a two-loansocial and labor programmatic operation to support the first two
pars of the newlyelected government was proposedinthe CountryAssistance Strategy (CAS), discussed
bythe Executive Directors onJanuary9,2003. However, due to the rapidpace of reforms inthe labor
and social sectors, a re-structuring of the Bank's portfolio for fiscal par 2006 as opportunities in the
countryshifted, andthe Government's request for further policysupport inthe social andlabor sectors, a
thirdlabor andsocial operation was recommendedinthe CASPR Since a thirdoperation hadnot been
anticipated, and no triggers were identified for a PLaARsSAL 111, this operation would necessarily be
stand-alone (Le. not part of the programmatic operation). However, it builds on the PLaRSSALs, as will
be demonstratedthroughoutthe text.

While this operation furthers the general goals identified inthe PLaRSSAL Iand I1- strengthen social
protection, raise humancapital formation, increase employment, and improve social oversight         -itis also
designed to consolidate the gains made duringthe Administration to allow for sustainability of reforms.
This closes the circle: the PLaRSSALI preparedduringthe firstparof the Administration focusedon
                                         -
developing the regulations for reforms, the PLaRSSALI1 prepared at the end of the second par of the
                                                              -                                   -
Administration focused onimplementationof the reforms, andthe LaRSDPLI11- prepared inthe last
                  -
18 months of the Administration - would focus on consolidation andsustainability of reforms.

The strategies, actions, andtargets described inthis document cover the period fromMarch2005,'2 when
the PLaRSSALI1closed, throughMarch2007, when this operation wouldclose. Bydesign, this spans an
electoral and a government transitionperiod so this operation would      (9continue to support the current
administration incompleting its reform agenda, and (ii) support the new administration to maintainand
buildongains of the 2002-2006 reformperiod.

The potential risks identified in the second operation in the PLaRSSAL did not occur, and those
identified for this operation are expected to be moderate if they are well managed. The continued
implementation sustainabilityof the labor andsocial reformthroughout the electoralprocess andintothe
new Administration face general risks, sector specific risks and transitionrisks. The genwal riks are the
same as those presented duriag earlier PLaRSSALs the violence, tight fiscal constraints, and external
                                                       -
political or economic shocks     - but  they may be exacerbated by the electoral process. The GoC is
mitigating these risks, with the support of the Bankandother donors, throughcontinued implementation
of the peace program, implementation of reforms to improve the fiscal situation, in-depth analysis of the
formula to redistribute resources to the sub-nationallevel, and careful management of relationships with
neighbors. The s a i d s a d ~ r
                               &riks       that may affect the sustainability of the reforms into the next
administration include the complexity of the reform process that could be challenging to manage,
sustaining actions with long-runbenefits that impose short-run costs, and pressure by special interest
groups who perceive that they maybe negativelyaffected bythe reform process. The GoC,with Bank
support, have implemented several strategies to address the risks: institutional change that would be
difficult to reverse, good monitoring and evaluation of programs to demonstrate the effectiveness of
reforms, s h a r q and discussing goals andresults withthe public so that civil society is better able to carry
forward the reformprograminto the next government, actions to increase revenues for use inthe social
sectors and increase program efficiency, and direct dialogue with public and private groups who feel
threatened by the reforms. The ~ m h & r i k           is the delay in the reform process as the President
forms his new government, but is considered minimalsince the President won re-election. This risk is
being mitigated bythe GoCvia a continued emphasis of the line Ministries on the same principles that
have guided them since 2002      -results-management, institutional reform, and fiscal austerity    - they
                                                                                                       as
finish their time inoffice.

                                     11.     COUNTRYCONTEXT

POLITICALCONTEXT
President Uribe was reelected in May 2006, with 62 percent of the vote, which was beyond the 50
percent threshold that would have required a run-offvote and greater than the 53 percent approval rating
he received in his first election in 2002.13 H i s popularityhas been largely attributed to his success in
addressing the peace issue, the improvement inaccess to socialservices, andthe gradualresurgence of the
economy, The President is expected to have greater support fromCongress inhis secondtermdue to his
party's and allies' success inthe March 2006 Congressional elections. This could facilitate the reform
process inthe second term


RECENTECONOMICDEVELOPMENTS
Colombia's economy has recovered since the start of the current CAS period (2002-2006); the CAS
Progress Report has extended this period throughthe end of 2007. The near stagnationof the economy
during the four years prior to the CAS has since given way to a sustained economic recovery and a
strengthening of consumer confidence.        The turnaround is partly due to the much improved global


12As muchas possible,datafromMarch2005 will be usedas a baseline. However, muchof the aggregationof
nationalstatistics is doneinDecember-for  the end of FYreports so inmanycases, that is the onlydataavailable,

inwhichcases,December2004indicatorswillbeusedas the baseline.

economic environment: world growth has accelerated, the cost of international credit has fallen, and the
prices of Colombia's primaryexports have risen. Domestic factors, particularly the improved security
situation and stable macroeconomic polices, have also driven the counuy's recovery.          Improving
conditions are reflected byan almost 4 percentage point increaseinprivate investment as a share of GDP
since 2002, and real economic growth that accelerated from 1.9 percent in2002 to 5.1 percent in2005.
Colombia's unemployment rate dropped from over 17 percent in 2002 to less than 10.4 percent in
December 2005. Inflation has stayed under control; falling from 7.0 percent in2002 to 4.9 percent in
2005 (see Table 1).
                            Table 1:KeyEconomicIndicatorsforColombia




       Ministeriode Hacienda,Bancode laRephblica,DANE,InternationalMonetaryFund;f: estimatedvalues.

Fiscal concerns persist, particularly budget rigidities, but the public sector deficit has improved
dramatically The combination of domestic economic growth, improved international conditions, peso
appreciation (which reduces the cost of servicing foreign currencydenominated debt), and revenue-
enhancing policy reforms has improved the fiscal accounts.       Policy reforms have concentrated on the
revenue side. In 2002, Congress approved Law 788, supported by the Bank's Fiscd and Institutional
Adjustment Loan (FIAL)Program14 This law sought to increase revenues and reduce tax distortions
throughthe elimination of several targeted exemptions. Overall, the reformbrought additional revenues
of 0.7 percent of GDP in2003 and about 1.1percent in2005. As a result, the combinedpublic sector
deficit decreasedfrom3.8 percent of GDPin2002 to a balance in2005.

Budget and legal rigidities that resist spending cuts have slowed the progress of policy reforms on the
expenditure side. These rigidities affect two main areas inwhich expendims have continued to grow
transfers to sub-national governments andthe pensions system In2002, the enactment of Law 715 set
the conditions for a more stable growthrate of transfers to sub-nationalgovernment mandatedunder the
Constitution, but this measure is set to be renewedin2007. From2008, the growthrate of transfers will
be calculated based onthe weighted average of the previous four years' GDP; further reformis needed if
the system is to become sustainable. Through a Technical Assistance Loan15 and additional IADB
funding, the Government is undertaung a study to determine the social impacts of the Law 715, as a
contribution to this discussion. Transfers to the mainstate-run pension system will continue to expand
over the next decade, as payouts greatly exceed new contributions. A constitutional reform, enacted in
June 2005, has made considerable progress in reducing pension liabilities over the medium term by
eliminating a series of special regimes for certain state employees andimposingcedmgs on benefits inthe
public-sector pension system The reform reduced the net present value of pension liabilities by 20.1
percentage points of GDP (from162 percent of GDP to about 143 percent). The originalbill aimed for
almosttwice this, butthe reformwas watered downinCongress.

MACROECONOMICOUTLOOKAND CHALLENGES
Inthe absenceof majorexternalshocks, the economyisexpected to continue growingat apacesimilar to
recent years.   Since prospects for significantly improving the factors supporting domestic demand


14 Loan 7163-c0, approvedMarch2003
15 Loan 726DC0, approvedNovember 2004

                                                      4

(internal peace, investment and credit) and external demand (exports and foreign direct investment) are
modest, it is likely that GDP growth will remain at around 4.0-5.0 percent through 2007.              Exports
continue to perform stronglx however, the rise in prices of Colombia's exports in recent years is not
likelyto continue and could even fallinthe next year or two. Foreigndirect investment           PI)flows    are
expected to increase with the likelycongressional approval of the Free Trade Agreement (FTA) as well as
with the enactment of recentbusiness environment reforms.

Colombia's level of public debt net of financial assets reached about 43 percent of GDP in2005. This
result further demonstrates the decreasing trend of this indicator since 2002 (54.5 percent of GDP).
Colombia's level of public debt is sustainable under most scenarios, appearing manageable inthe near
termevenwhenconsideringpossible adverseeconomic shocks.

Export markets will generate uncertainty inthe coming months. Oilprices will continue to have major
effects on the economythrough their impact on export and fiscal revenues and as a keydeterminant of
growth in Venezuela, the countrjs main market after the US for non-traditional exports.                    The
implementation of the FTA, which could boost trade between the Andean countries and the US, still
depends on approval bythe US Congress andthe ColombianCongress andConstitutionalCourt.

POVERTYAND INEQUALITY

Even as a countrywith a longhistoryof solid growthand stable democratic institutions, povertyremains
a critical issue for Colombia. The WorldBank's 2002 PovertyAssessment foundthat years of consistent
economic growth lifted many out of poverty, but the macroeconomic crisis at the end of the 1990s
eradicated more than a decade of progress. By 2002, in the aftermath of the crisis, 58 percent of the
populationwas living below the poverty line (Figure         1)16and 20 percent were considered extreme poor
(Figure2).17 Inruralareas, povertyrates reached 76 percent. Colombia alsohas one of the mostunequal
income distributions inthe world      -the   poorest20 percent of the populationreceives just 3 percent of the
nation's income.'*

Withthe returnof stable economic growth inthe last fewyears, nationalpovertymeasures have begunto
improve again. The nationwide povertyrate dropped to 50 percent by2005, the extreme povertyrate fell
to 15 percent, and rural poverty returned to 1996 levels (althoughstill lugh at 69 percent).19 After
rea&        10 percent in 1999, the share of the populationliving on less than US$1 a day is down to 3.1
percent, puttingColombia on track to reach its Millennium Development Goals (MDGs)for poverty?o
Similarly, the percentage of people living on less than US$2 a day fell from 18 percent in 1999 to 7.7
percent six years later.

                             111.      THE GOVERNMENT'SPROGRAM




The Government's guiding principles have been the National Development Plan (NDP) and the
Millennium Development Goals (MDGs). The NDPpresents the Government's longtermvisionas well
as the strategy for the four years of the Uribe Administrationto contribute toward attainiq that vision.
The vision for each sector supported bythis operation is discussed inSection V below, but the overall
objectives of the Government for 2002-2006 are91 security, sustainable economic development and
employment generation, social equiv, and transparency and efficiency of the State.         The strategy to
achieve these goals includes an aggressivesocial andlabor programthat puts the countrywell on the path
to achieving its MDGs. Table 2 shows that Colombia is ontrack inachieving its nationalMDGs inall
categories, except m a t e dhealth.U

Although the NDP and MDGs present a long-termvision, the GoChas given new attention to better
defining this vision. Inanticipation of Colombia's 200th anniversary of their independence inthe year
2019, the NationalP-             Department (DNP        -              N      ddePt$znw&) launchedaninitiative
in2004 todevelop alongtermvisionforthecountryandastrategyforreachingit. Theprocess involves
the development andpublishmg of a proposalbythe DNP Vi& W II C&mznix 2019 prqbtdesta
                                                                    -                                      -
panz fimih,followed bya roundof discussions with citizens andsub-nationalgovernments to refine the
plan and to generate national ownership.             The proposal was published in 2005 and identifies four
development objectives for the long run? an economy that guarantees greater levels of well-being, a
societyof free andresponsible citizens, a more egalitarian society, anda State that serves it citizens.

The third objective a more egalitarian society specificallyrelates to the labor and social sectors. The
                        -                               -
primaryobjective is to close the social gaps interms of life expectancy, healthinsurance, infantmortality,
early childhood development, primaryand secondary school attendance, average educational attainment,
unemployment rates, andpoverty. The proposed strategiesfor achieving this are:

         Permanently increase efficiency in social expenditure so that more resources can be translated
         intobetter outcomes inqualityandcoverage;

         Continuouslyimprove targeting of expenditures;

         &ate      the necessary information to target expenditures t o d those most in need, monitor
         results andundertake periodic evaluations of programs, andsharethe informationwiththe public
         inathelymanner, and

         Consolidate the SPS (Social Protection System), which aims to protect the poorest and most
         vulnerable, throughthe development of prevention instruments and social assistance that would
         contribute to strengthening and protecting human capital and household income and reduce
         vulnerabilities.

The fourth general objective a State that serves it citizens includes efforts for better governance inthe
                                  -                                 -
social and labor sectors, among others, to create an efficient and transparent State. The strategy for
achieving this is to improve mechanisms for effective citizen participation such that a process exists
where citizens maydefine, execute, monitor, andevaluate the functions of the State.
While the Vis& 2019 covers all sectors, the Government is puttingadditional emphasis on the challenge
of povertyreduction. InJanuary2004, the DNPcreated a Commissionfor the Design of a Strategy for
the Reduction of Poverty and Inequality (MERPD,M i & p a la Estmttgia de Rfduadnde la P                         k y la
hi&,           whichbroughttogether members of Colombiangovernment, academia,the WorldBank,the
Inter-American Development Bank (IADB), the United Nations Development Program (UNDP), and
the Economic Commission for LatinAmerican and The Caribbean (ECLACJ. The MERPD is charged
with identifying the sources of povertyand inequality inColombia, evaluating the role of the public and
private sectors inpovertyalleviation, andproposinga long-term, multi-sectoral, multi-agencystrategyfor
combating poverty and inequality in the country.             The Bank is supporting the process by providing
technical and financial support to the consultation process, preparing and providingtechnical assistance
for the backgroundstudies, andparticipatinginthe crafung of the finaldiagnostic andstrategypaper.

                 JY.     BANKSUPPORTTOTHE GOVERNMENT'SSTRATEGY

LINKTOCAS
The CAS, discussed bythe Boardof Directors onJanuary9,2003, describes the Bank's core strategyfor
Colombia, and hlghlghts three main goals: fast and sustainable growth; sharing the fruits of growth; and
qualitygovernment institutions. A CAS Progress Report, discussed on September 29, 2005, validates
these three goals and adds two areas of assistance. First, a fourth "pillar" was added to Bank's strategyto
support the GoCs efforts to buildthe foundations for peace. Second, the Bankstrategy nowincludes a
monitoring and evaluation (Ma)prong to support the Government's                     efforts to implement
performance-basedmanagement inthe public sector.

The proposed LaRSDPLI11directlycontributes to achieving the mainobjectives of the CAS:

        Admug Fast and Szstainablegnmh, To achieve faster and sustained growth, Colombia will need
        to increase employment of workers as well as growth intotal factor productivity. Initiatives to
         increase employment opportunities were supported by the PLaRSSAL and are a part of the
        proposed LaRSDPL 111. Moreover, the education and training reforms advanced by the
         government and nurturedbythe PLaRSSALs and the proposedLaRSDPL I11increase the pace
         of the country`s human capital accumulation and push private sector productivityt o 4 the
         technological frontier. Inthe Bank's report, CYairqgdeGcpinE&            ard T*t5       these two
         conditions are deemed necessaryfor sustained and rapid economic growth inthe technologically
         changmg globaleconomy.

         Shanrsp de+       cfgmztth. The labor, education, training, social assistance, and health reforms
         supported bythe LaRSDPLI11enhance the ability of allpeople, especially the poor, to share in
         the fruits of economic growth. Employment is the single m o s t importantsource of income for
         poorpeople. Hence, reforms that expand opportunitiesinthe labor market andincreasepeople's
         preparedness for productive employment are likely to reduce poverty and income equality.
         Improved cost-effectiveness and targeting of early childhood and job training programs to low-
         income people, called for under the LaRSDPL 111, will also reduce inequalities in people's
         welfare. Moreover, progress toward universal access to good qualitybasic education andhealth
         services promoted bythe PLaRSSAL and the LaRSDPL I11will provide the greatest benefits to
         lower income childrenandtheir families.

         BEalduspqz&v    gpemmm~. The LaRSDPL 111, as the PLaRSSALs before it, contributes to the
         development of quality government by supporting reforms intended to promote the
         transparency, social accountability, coordination, and rationalization of keysocial programs and
         institutions (e.g. MSP, ICBF, SEN& and MEN). In addition, the operation enhances the
         opportunities for participation of Colombia's citizens.     Such actions are expected to reduce
         inefficiencies andimprove relations betweenthe populationandthe GoC

         P t w ~ A critical element of buildingfoundations for peace is ensuring adequate social supports

         protection system    - including program coordination; targeting programs to the poor, and
         information, monitoringand evaluation systems       -willenhance  the country`s capacityto provide
         basic education, health services, and social assistance and insurance to the poor, i n c l h the
         displaced, recently demobilized populations, and those living in ex-conflict zones.26

COLLABORATIONWITHTHE IMPAND OTHERDONORS

IMF. An 18 monthStand-ByAgreement betweenColombia andthe IMFwas signed inApril, 2005 for
SDR 405 million (approximately $580 d o n ) . The agreement will be treated as precautionary, as a
transition to the end of further IMF support. The most recent programreview verified that the program
was on track (Annex 111). Infact, during 2005, Colombia strongly outperformed the programtargets.
The IMF Colombia team has been consulted during the preparation of this operation to ensure
consistencywiththe benchmarks specified inthe current IMFagreement withColombia.

The Inter-American Development Bank. The IADB and the Bank have worked together closely to
support Colombia's socialsectors. This partnership beganin1999, when the IADB andthe Bankjointly
supported the F a d k en A d and the EnpSo en A&                projects; and the partnership continued
throughoutthe life of these operations via joint supervision missions andtechnical assistance. Gu-rently,
the IADB is managing three social loans which complement and are complemented bythe work being
supported by the Bank The Health Reform operation is designed to support the Ministry of Social
Protection inthe development of thek health beneficiaries data base, while the Bank's Social Safety Net
InvestmentLoan27is supportingthe creation of this type of data base for the other programs under the
MSP's purview. Two Hospital Restructuring projects are also worlung to improve efficiency in the
hospitalsector. An IADB operation to support the r e - s d g of the immunizationprogram, signed
inSeptember2005, picks upwherethePLaRSSALI1leftoff, andwillprovideinvestmentbudgettofund
actions that the Bank's policy loans could not address. In addition, the IADB is preparing its first
Programmatic Policy Operation for the social sectors in Colombia, which will focus on increasing the
equity of social expenditure; the first operation inthe series is expected to be presented to the IADB's
BoardinJune 2006. The IADB has also beenworkingwith the ICBFandMinistryof Education inthe
development of anEarlyChildhoodDevelopmentstrategy, as wellas supported the impact evaluations of
the earlychildhoodprograms. The IADBhas providedcrucialfinancial andtechnical support to the GoC
inits creation of data bases to improve targeting of socialservices, namelyhugh supportto the 2006
Census andto the systemthat classifies the povertylevelof households (SISBEV. The Bankhas closely
coordinated with the IADB in all these initiatives, and particularlywith the IADB's programmatic loan
t e a m

The Andean Development Corporation(CAF). CAF,the WorldBank,andthe Ministryof Education

  Performance (SINERGIA) was implemented inall 16 sector ministries and 170 public entities. Under this
  system Indicative Plans are established for each sector and entity for each par.The system has been in
  operation since 1997. Performance evaluations of the sector ministries are carried out bythe DNP.The
  entities carry out self-evaluation and are also evaluated by their respective ministries. An information
  systemto managethe evaluation systemhas also beenimplemented.

POLICYAREAS
SocialProtection
MainIssues &Longtermvision. Colombia's success inavoidmgsystemic macroeconomic shocks during
most of its modem history hindered the creation of an integrated social protection system Instead,
programs were developed inresponse to immediate needs of the heterogeneous population, resulting ina
fragmented social protection system with a wide range of institutionsat the national, territord, andlocal
levels; a hodgepodge of public and private strategies; and an absence of cohesive policies, clearlydefined
objectives, adequate targeting, impact evaluation, and efficient financing schemes. Despite significant
efforts during the 1990s to increase coverage in basic education and health insurance, by 2002, there
remained deficits incoverage, access, andqualityof services for the ill,the vulnerable, those who become
unemployed or needto improve their skills for greater success inthe job market, andfor the poor.

The GoCs long-termvisionis to close the social and regional gaps so that allColombians have access to
h& qualitybasic services andprotection against keysocial andeconomic risks that would allowthemto
carry out activities necessary for their personal and social development. This would be achieved by
buildinga social protection system that contributes to strengthening and protecting humancapital and
household income, giving effective support in situations of crisis, and helping poor and vulnerable
families.37

In2002, the Pastrana Administration approved &io             N      d de Pdi& Ecwhia y Social (National
Council of Economic and Social Policy, CONI'ES)38 documents 3144 and 3187 outhing a Social Risk
Management Framework (Stctemt Social de Risgo, SSR) and Social Protection System (SPS), which:             (9
established a framework for socialprotectionpolicies focusing on riskprevention, mitigation, and coping
mechanisms, as highhghtedinthe Bank's S            w c w Futrazt9 and Clhdnz S       d S&   N@ reports; (q
called for a reorganizationof RAS programs, ICBF (I11sth.a~Ghrrhimde BienstmF d i w
Institute for Family Well-being),
Traungl, andother agencies to respondto socialprotection needs; and (3assignedto these programs
                                     SENA (S&          N      d de A&e           -              -Colombian
                                                                                   National Service for Job

and agencies social protection functions that address both crisis periods and chronically poor
populations.41

Government Stratepu. The Uribe Administration views social protection as the efficient and equitable
management of social risks andtheir consequences. Ithas a two-pronged strategy based onthe CONPES
approved inthe previous administration: institutionalreformto allowfor better integration amongpublic
agencies, coherent policies, and less duplication of efforts; and creation of an SPS to reduce the risks
caused by economic shocks and coordinate public policyto address the harmfuleffects of poverty and
vulnerability. The strategy would be achieved by creating a Ministry of Social Protection (MSP) and
designing and implementing the SPS, incollaboration with the institutions mappedto the MSP, inorder
to consolidate the manysocialprotectionprogramsunder one umbrella. 42

Actions Supported under PLaRSSALIandPLaRSSAL11. The Ministryof SocialProtection (MSP) was
legallycreated underLaw 789 inDecember, 2002, andestablished inJanuary2003, throughthe merger of

Actions sumortedbythe LaRSDPL11144

PriarAcricpaardBendnmk5. Improve theMSP'sabilityto increasesocialprotectioncoverage and
efficiencythrough:    (9 issuance   of regulationsto facilitate broaderaccess to social security benefits,

I-              $Priar A     h ard Bendnmk Aciiam,a dRe~ultrtoDate. In2005/2006, the GoC instituted
two regulations to increase access to social protection programs among workers and eliminate
disincentives to formal sector employment. Before October 2005, workers inthe Subsidized Regime45
would lose their insurance three months after joining the formal sector since their employers were
expected to pay into the ContributoryRegime on their behalf. However, if they then lost their formal
sector job, they returned to queue for access to the SubsidizedRegime, which could take manyyears, thus
leaving them andtheir families without health insurance.46 Anecdotal evidence suggests workers regularly
refused formalsector job offers for fear of losingtheir spot inthe Subsidized Regime. A newregulation47
allows workers to preserve their eligibility for affiliation to the Subsidized Regime for up to one year after
accepting a formal sector job, thus getting workers through the riskyfirst year of employment?* It is
expected that this decree willdecreasethe incentives for informalsector employment.

A second regulation allows for individualworkers who otherwise do not participate inthe socialsecurity
system   -whetherdueto      their status as self-employed workers or as informalsector employees         -to  have
greater access to the Contributory Health Regime, pensions system, and/or occupational hazard
insurance.49 While Law 100 of 1993 allowed for independent workers to buyinto the systems, the cost
was high since they were required to pay the full value of benefits, which is otherwise shared between
employee and employer. The new regulation lowered the cost of buying into the Social Security System
andis expected to expandsocialprotectioncoverage to those who couldnotaffordaccess, andto loosen
the links betweenlabor force status andaccess to social benefits.




43 Before the merger  of the Ministries of Labor and Health into the Ministryof Social Protection, social protection
issues related to health and pensions were the responsibility of the Ministry of Health, while those related to

spanned boththe health sector and the labor market, such as the ability to maintainhealth coverage/benefits across
different jobs and/or employment states, didnot belongto either Ministryand, thus, were not adequatelyaddressed.

Bythe end of the operation, the prior action inthis section - issuanceof regulations to facilitate broader
access to social security benefits  - is expected to increase access of independent workers to the social
securitysystem andto increase coverage of health benefits among a mobilelabor force.

The MSP created two information systems to increase efficiency in the provision of social protection
services. The Integrated Benefits Payment System ( P a Integradz de L+                      de Aparts - PILA),
allows employen to submit monthlypayroll taxes directlythrough a webbasedsystem, which significantly
reduces employer's transaction costs, increases the efficiency of labor tax collection to finance social
programs, and creates a d i e d data base of workers. The PILA was launched inmid-2005, and, as of
April 2006, more than 5600 employers use the system to paybenefits of more than400,000 employees.
This number is expected to increase bythe end of the operation, as the new MSP team continues to
encourage use of the PILA.

The UnifiedRegistryof Affiliates (RUN Regirtro
                                            -         LirnJicl?dodeAflizh) is a   databasethat would track all
beneficiaries of social insurance and social assistanceprograms inthe country. It is expected to buildthe
foundations of the integrated SPS since it will allow the Colombian authorities to keep track of
beneficiaries and their benefits, enabling the MSP to more rigorouslymonitor and enforce eltgibility of
beneficiaries under the different programs. This registryhas been designed and will be implemented by
July2006. Bythe end of the operation, the MSPwillbe inthe process of collecting andinputtingdata to
the system.

Emected lone-run&acts         of actions sumorted bvthe DPL. Inthe long run, the new regulations are
expected to increase formal sector employment, particularlyin temporary jobs, since the risk of losing
health insurance  -andthustheriskofpovertythroughillness-islower.              Transitorypovertyand/or old-
age poverty of poor independent workers should also be reduced since they can better affordto payinto
the ContributoryRegime, occupational hazard,andpensions systems.

The regulation for affiliation byindependent workers to the health system should not impose a cost on
the Ministry unless adverse selection dominates, that is, unless affiliation of independent workers is
dominated by those who need to draw more out of the system than they put in. The economic and
financial importance of adverse selection is notkuownand, thus, willhave to be monitored.

While the creation of the information systems will create up-front costs, they should have long-run
savings. The PILA will increase the financial resources available for buildingthe SPS, since it will collect
allpayrolltaxes from each workefl andeliminate the practice of declaring different income bases for
different taxes.51 The data base of employen and workers will also provide the information to better
determine the optimallevel of taxation necessaryto payworker benefits. The RUN will allow for the
MSP to better target social insurance and protectionprograms and to monitor howthe public uses the
SPS andhowthe systemcanbeimproved.

LaborandHealth. A recent report shows that efficiency gains have been achieved since the mergingof
the ministries throughthe reduction of staff bymore than 15 percent d          e increasing service coverage by
19percent.52

The MSP has also been forming partnerships with other institutions to better coordinate the SPS. In

                                                                            -
SENA (the training institutionmapped to the MSP) to improve the qualityand coverage of Colombia's
education and training institutions by jointly defining criteria for accreditation of institutions, creating
mechanisms to allow for students to move between MSP and MENeducational institutions, designing
education programs to better respond to the labor market, and worktng to involve the private sector in

The primarysocial assistanceprogramfor poor, nu-al families           -F   d k enA&        -continues to provide
cash transfers to extremelypoor families whose children regularly attend school and health centers. At
present, approximately one million children from extremely poor families (SISBEN I) benefiting.   are
This program, which was created in and continues to be run by the Social Action (A&                        Sad)
Department of the Presidency, is experimenting with the feasibility of operating in even more remote
areas (specificallythose without banks) andinmarginalurbanareas. Three urbanpilots are underway and
one (Medellin) is accompanied byan impact evaluation team; three non-bankqpilots are also underway.
These initiative benefit 30,000 and 10,000 families, respectively, inadditionto the original340,000 families
that have participated inthe program since its inception in2001. The pilots contribute to a long term
goalof expanding the programto 1.5 d o nfamilies by2019.53

Inaddition, the Presidencyisimplementingapilotusingamechanismsimilar to that developed underthe
F d k enAcuikprogram, but directed toward families registered inthe NationalRegistryfor Displaced
Populations.~In 2006, 100,000 displaced families across Colombia are expected to benefit from a
conditional cash transfer designed to stabilize their families and homes. This initiative is financed
exclusivelybygovernment funds.

On the pension front, a Constitutionalreform55 lead bythe MHCand the MSP, was approved in2005
with the objective of movingforwardthe process to reduce long-term pension obligations.56 They key
reforms were the elimination of special regimes       - thus  regularizing the pension rules across the formal
andpublic sectorlabormarkets, eliminating the 14thpaymentfor highsalaryworkers withthe intentionof
cost savings and reducing the regressivity of the system, and establishing a ceiling on the size of pension
payments, also to address the financing and regressive tax issues (most of the reforms take effect in2010-
11). Despite the gains inthis hard-foughtreform, the operational deficit of the RPMsystem andother
public pensionregimes is expected to remain at 4-5 percent of GDP over the next 20 years.57

EarlyChildhoodDevelopment(ECD)
MainIssues &Loflp termvision. Years of violence, inadditionto the economic crisis at the end of the
90s,contributedto the critical situation of Colombia's children ages 0-6. Colombiasuffered froma lack of
planning and supervision among a diverse portfolio of ECD programs and approaches, with poor
targeting, little information about program effectiveness and results, and poor collaboration with or
control at the localleve1.58

The Government's long-termvisionfor the sector is to protect and develop all children's potenual, with
special attention given to the most vulnerable. The ColombianInstitute for FamilyWelfare (ICBF) is the
institutionresponsible for coordinating andimplementing most of the programs designed to improvethe
living conditions of vulnerable children. Through ICBF programs, Famllias tmAaGn, and Ministry of
Education, the GoChopes to support the poorest childrenreach a minimumlevel of nutrition,cognitive
development, andschool readiness andsuccess.

Government Stratew. The Uribe Administration has made specific efforts to improve andexpand ECD
programs with a special focus on poor children.The Government's strategy for the period 2002-2006
with regards to ECD is to increase access of the poorest children to lvgh quality ECD services by
implementation of a pro-poor targeting policy, increasing coverage of ICBF nutrition programs by
400,000 children, andimplementationof practices to ensure the cost-effectiveness of ECDprograms. It
has made targeting and monitoring and evaluation the center pieces of its institutional development
strategy, with a new emphasis on better linkages with the subnational level to further increase coverage
andqualityof services.

Reflecting the Institute's targeting policy; ICBF has reallocated more than six percent of the program's
resources for 2006 to departments with high incidence of vulnerable children, four percentage points
more than the target set at appraisal. This was achieved byinstituting a targeting strategy based on the
gap between the coverage and need, starting with pre-school children. Those municipalities with more
vulnerable children and greater gaps incoverage received a higher proportionof the resources. Once all
needypre-school children were guaranteed a school lunch, the eligibilitywas extended to children inthe
first grade, andthe process continuedthrough the thirdgrade. This leadto anincrease incoverage of the
school feedmg programfrom 30 to 37 percent between 2005 and 2006, a reallocation of funds toward
younger children, disproportionately hqgh increase in funds for the (conflict affected) northern
departments (states), and an increase in pre-school matriculation by more than 200,000 children. The
end-of-operation results is that the reallocated resources are secured throughout N 0 6 , and across the
transition period.

A report produced bythe ICBF and the Attorney General's Office, with support of UNICEF and the
IADBM showed the unsystematic inclusion of early childhood investments in municipal development
plans. Inresponse, the ICBF sponsored several sub-national conferences to discuss results and agree

Health
MainIssues &Longterm vision. Until1993,Colombia had a health systemwhich, like most inLatin
America, relied on publicly operated facilities to provide services to the uninsured population, which
encompassed most of Colombia's poor.65 This system was fahg to meet the needs of poor people, as
demonstrated bylowhealth services utilizationandaccess figures bythe early 1990s.

In1993,the GoCinitiateda shift towarda system that wouldutilizepublic funds to provide subsidized
insurance to poorpeople (Subsidized Health Insurance Regime) byprovidmgthem withanentitlement to
a specified list of services while workers inthe formalsector independent of povertystatus
                                                                    -                                     -wouldpay
into a ContributoryHealth Insurance Regime via payroll taxes. Such insurance-based entitlements are a
more effective means to ensure access to health services, especially in highly resourceconstrained
systems.% The new svucture also promoted efficiency because health care providers must provide
services to people inorder to receive payment frominsurers.

While the new system created the means to ensure access (insurance) and efficiency (output based
reimbursement), impact on utilization, and hence health outcomes, relies on the breadth of insurance
coverage. As of 2002, only55 percent of the populationhadhealth insurance, a decreasefrom61 percent
in1998.67

Immunizationcoverage, a keypublic healthissueandafundamental measureto preventpovertyinducing
illness, has been a challenge for Colombia. In1995/6, vaccination coverage rates were above 90 percent
for allkeyvaccines, thus providing "herd immunitf so that if apersondidcontract anillness, asufficient
amount of the populationwas vaccinated to prevent an epidemic. However, between 1996 and 2001,
average coverage rates rangedbetween72 and77percent, putting Colombia at a serious publichealth risk
The dramatic fall inimmunization rates was attributed to inadequate funding and organizational changes
associated with decentralization, as well as the shift      to an insurance-based system that generated
uncertainties about responsibilities for implementing and overseeing various components of the
immunizationprogram

The GoCs longterm vision is 100 percent of the population is covered byhealth insurance by2019 and
95 percent of all children age 0-5 are vaccinated. This would be funded byincreasing efficiencies inthe
health system byclosing under-utilized hospitals, increasing qualitystandards andmonitoring health care
providers, s

Government Stratevy. The Uribe Government has placed a priorityon expanding insurance coverage for
poorpeople ina resource constrainedenvironment. The strategyfor achieving this duringthe 2002-2006
period includes the mobilizationof additional funds to finance an expansion of the Subsidized Health
Regime from 11.4 million to 13.7 million beneficiaries, conhued scallng down of public funding of
hospitaland reallocation of funds to demand-driven health insurance, and achieving efficiency gains in
health care provision,primarilythroughrestructuring 100hospitals.

Vital to the reform process is maintaining coverage and quality of services as it expands access to
healthcare.     This involves the establishment of a quality assurance system that uses licensing and
accreditationof providers as wellas oversees the overalleffectiveness of the regulatoryframework

The primaryfocus of the Government's actions with respect to immunizationis to increase immunization
rates among childrenage 0-5 by ensuring predictable levels of funding and clarifying responsibilities for
implementing and overseeing various components of the immunizationprogram.

Actions Supported under PLaRSSAL Iand PLaRSSAL 11. Between 2002 and 2004, the GOC made
significant and steady progress inexpandmg the coverage of the subsidized insurance regime from 11.4
millionto 15.5 millionpoor people,bs compared to the GoCs four year target of 13.7 million and the
PLaRSSALI&I1target of 11.5 million. Evidence to date indicates that obtainingthis insurance coverage
is increasing access to health care for poor people, as well as reducing the financial risk associatedwith
fallingill,andhavingtopayfor care.69

One of the key sources of budget to fund the expansion of the Subsidized Regime came from the
reductioninthe portionof the budget flowing to hospitals as supplysubsidies. Due to the expansion in
insurance coverage (bothsubsidized and contributoryregime), hospitals received more income based on
services provided. The shift from input to output-based financing r e d hospitals for productivity
gains. The Government also maintained a program to support hospital restructuring to help them

have the resources to absorb them since it was alreadyunder-fundedand healthresources were frozen duringthe
crisis-ratherthanexpandedina      counter-cyclicalmanner as theywere usedto meet generalfinancial conditions.

 re&     for the poor shouldhave significant impacts on povertyinthe short and long run. Inthe short

Other ~ r o ~ r e sinsthe G E s broader labor and social reformprogram The Government continues to
roll-out q d t y initiatives, ind+        efforts in licensing and accreditation that have licensed 1,499
hospitals, as well as 43,018 health care professionals (e.g. physicians; nurses). In 2005, four public
hospitals and three private hospitals achieved accredited status. Accreditation standards are under
preparation for other types of facilities, incl+         ambulatory care facilities and clinical laboratories.
Another 71hospitals entered the res-                programin2005, reachq 124 hospitals that have entered
or completedthe process, representinga substantialstep toward increasing the qualityof the care inthese

                                                        28

facilities while increasing efficiency and generating cost savings, and surpassing the GoCs 2006 goal of
100 hospitals restructured.

The hospitalrestructuring process has been slower than it could be due to resistance from those who
would be affected bythe process. Inparticular,when hospitals close, some staff is reallocated to other
hospitals that pickup the demand fromthe closed hospitals, but other staff are laidoff. The process of
negotiating with the workers for a suitable compensationpackage takes time and is very expensive inthe
short run. However, the combined ministries of labor and health, inthe form of the MSP, have the
expertise andthe meansto negotiate this process.

Colombia was able to achieve substantialimprovements inimmunizationcoverage in2002 and2004, but,
likemanycountries, has founditdifficult to obtainhighercoverage, especially in"hardto reach" areas75
As a result, the Government undertook a detailed analysis of the immunizationprogram's structure and
functioning in 2004, which found that the key constraints on program effectiveness were: stable and
adequate funding; vaccine supply, quality and distribution; and weak capacity in the management and
administration of immunbtion activities. To address these issues, the Government is implementing a
project with IADB and CAF resources (US$133.7 million), including a commitment to adequate and
stable fundingfor the immunizationprogramthrough2010.

The MSPhas an active programof discussionanddebate withcivil societythroughvarious activities, thus
enabkng "social oversight" of Governments policies and results inhealth. Participation inthese fonuns
has beenbroad andrepresentative. See Annex IVfor apresentation of events andinitiatives.

LaborandTraining

MainIssues & Long term vision. When the Uribe Administration took office in2002, Colombia was
slowly emerging from the 1998/99 economic crisis. Economic growth was positive, but low, and the
unemployment rate was still near 17 percent.          Women and youth were hit particularly hard by
unemployment, as labor participation of these groups increased to compensate for the loss in family
income, when household heads lost their jobs.76 Safety nets for unemployed workers were absent,
increasing the hardship of andpovertyimplications of beingunemployed.

Inthelongrun,thevisionistoreformlegislationtoreducedisincentives toemployment, accompanied by
a series of socialprotectionschemes to protectthe unemployed andto improve the employabilityof hard-
to-employ groups (youth, women, unskilled, rural).         This entails identifying and modifying those
regulations that prevent employers from h r q , b d d q a basket of labor-related social protection
schemes, designing and implementing the National System for Job Training (SNFT, S i t m N             d &
F         d para d Tmhqzlo),to increase the quality and supply of training opportunities, and expand the
apprenticeship programto facilitate labor force entryfor the young.

Government Strateg. The Uribe Government's strategy has focused on design and implementation of
labor and training reforms to reduce the wedge between employers' hurng/trahing cost and workers'
acceptable wage, while improvingthe socialprotection of vulnerable workers andinitiatingthe process to
create the SNFT. The key vehicles have been Government-introduced measures to the labor code to
reduce the costs of labor to firms, and improve and expand training programs for workers to increase
their employability and productivityinthe market place, primarilythrough the Labor Reform Law 789
andCONPES081(&go             N      ddePd& Ecrmhkizy Sorial- NationalCouncilof EconomicandSocial
Policy). President Uribe has also been particularlykeen to pilot programs to support individuals inthe
formalandinformalsectors who become unemployed.



75 "Had-to-reach" areas includeboththose that are remoteandthose that areunderthe controlof non-government
forces, andthus cannot be accessedbygovernment healthworkers.

Actions Supported under PLaRSSAL Iand PLaRSSAL 11. To improve economic performance and
employment generation, the government introducedmeasures to the labor code to reduce the labor costs
of firms andimprovetraining programs for workers to increasetheir employabilityandproductivityinthe
market place. This included the passing of labor reform law 789 of December 2002 and its regulatory
decrees, which introduced changes to the labor code to reduce the cost of overtime pay, correct the non-
linearities in the severance system that created perverse firing decisions;"      reduce the cost of apprentice
contracts and of hard-to-employ workers, and introduce measures to improve social protection of
workers. Followingthis law, CONPES No. 81 of July2004 was approved, which outlined the initialsteps
to create the SNFT.78

By December 2004, Law 789 was being implemented and much progress had been achieved inlabor
market performance and onkeyindicators affectedbythe labor reformlaw 789. Specifically.

     O p e n unemployment decreased from nearly 16 percent in2002 to 12.1 percent (annualaverage) by
     December 2004. While this is partlydue to resumed economic growth, the decrease inthe cost of
     labor andthe added flexibilityto labor contracts also played apart, namelythroughthe lengthening of
     the "regular work day",79 reduction of holiday pay, and ahgning of severance payments.80             The
     government estimates that employment increased by nearly 1.3 million workers between 2002 and
     2004, short of their target of 1.8 million workers for the period 2002-2006, but still significant,
     particularly given the slow resumption of economic growth since the crisis. A large part of this
     increase appears to have been in formal employment, as indicated by the rise in affiliation to
     occupational hazardinsurance programs bynearly900,000 workers andaffiliationto the contributory
     health insurance regime by nearly 1.7 million people (contributors and their dependents), between
     2002 and 2004. An impact evaluationshows that the rise informality can be attributed to the labor
     reform, as can the reductioninunderemployment.81

     The reformof the apprenticeship contract- whereby employers can hire low-skilledapprentices for a
     s i x monthperiod paying them 50 percent of the minimumwage during a three-month trainkg period
     and 75 percent of minimum wage during the three months of practice-has                 yielded impressive
     results. The annual number of apprentices hired more thandoubledfrom33,000 annuallyin2002 to
     74,000 annuallyin2004 with a cumulative gainof 146,000 over the two-year period, relative to a GoC
     target of 288,000 for the four year period. More than 16,000 were graduates of private sector uaining
     firms rather than SENA, thus contributing to the creation of the National System of Job Training
     (SNFT) byexpanding beyondSENA andto include private sector actors.

     While CONPES 081 was being regulated, SENA undertook short-term processes to expand
     Colombia's job training network to the private sector. By2004, SENA had approved 171training
     comes to complement those offered by SENA, whereas no private sector training comes had
     SENA's approval ratingin2002.

Improvements in social protection in the labor market are shown by the fact that more than 110,000
unemployment subsidies were provided bythe CCF in2005,w against a target of 100,000 annuallyset by
the GoCand bythe LaRSDPL 111. Notably, 46 percent of those receiving subsidies were informalsector
workers with no previous affiliation to the Czjk This is an increase from 2004, when the share of
informal sector beneficiaries was capped at 36 percent.        Of the recipients, 62 percent are women,
reflecting the higher unemployment rates of this group. Approximately67 percent are ages 30-50, which
is notthe age group with the highest unemployment rates (youthare), but mayreflect the role of this age
group as householdhead, andthus the need to provide household income even when unemployed. The
endof projectgoalis to maintainthe targets.

Provision of unemployment subsidies are expected to improve job matches since unemployed workers
have resources to conduct a more thorough job search. Also, it should prevent the negative welfare
shocks experienced duringjob loss and shoulddecrease the incentive to pullchildren out of school and
into the labor force as the household copes withjob loss. The unemployment subsidyis still a pilot, and
funded by general revenues, but if expanded, the GoC will need to explore means to reallocate less
efficiently used social protectionfunds towardthe program.

The reforms of SENA and, particuhly,of the labor reformhave been difficult, but keypublic institutions
are workingwith concerned parties to advance the reformprocess. Inparticular,the GoCrecognizes the
importantrole that SENAplays bothinsocietyandinthe labor market. Thus, it is w o r k with SENA
as a partner to create a National Job Trainjng System, building on SENA's expertise inthe field d             e
making reforms to take SENA, and the SNFT, into the globaliwng economy. The labor reform
introduced some difficult changes for some workers       -suchas     adjusting the severance payrates    -butit
has simultaneouslyattempted to offer measures to counter-balance negative events that may result, such
as the unemployment subsidypilot.

Other Drozress inthe G E s broader labor and social reform program. Since 2002, the unemployment
rate  &   dicreased from 16 percent to 10.4 percent thereby reaching the NDP's unemployment rate
targets. While this decline cannot be completely attributed to the labor reforms, Law 789 can take some
credit.92

SENAhas also beenmakrng concerted efforts for efficiency gains inthe administrative budget. The size
of the permanent staff was reduced by 7.7 percent, but output did not decline since the reduction in
personnel was offset byincreasing the workload of staff from26 to 32 hours weekly and from42 to 45
weeks annuallyand bysub-contracting 15.6 percent of its t e a c h staff. These r e f o m are estimated to
have saved US$23million,which was usedto increasethe coverage andqualityof programs.

Education
Main Issues & Lo? term vision.             Despite significant increases in education spending and the
implementation of decentralization policies in the 1990s, and increasing coverage of education,
enrollments at the primaryand secondary school levels are not yet universal. The average educational
level of Colombians 15 year and older increased from 6.4 in 1992 to 7.6 in2003, but significant regional
disparities exist: inregional capitals, the average level of schooling is 8.5 years, but ody4.96 years inthe
rest of the country. There is gender paritybetween men and women. The schooling level of the adult
Colombianpopulationcontinues to lagbehindcomparator countries inthe region.

Interms of schoolcompletion, 2004 datarevealthat only61percentof childrenwho start primaryschool
finishthe fifth grade, while 57 of those who begin secondary school finish ninth grade and only 35
percent complete eleventh grade. Poorresults onthe SABER (student performance) tests show the need
to undertake actions to improve the quality of education.        As with other education indicators, large
differences are seen not onlybetweenpublicandprivate schools, but also across rural andurbanareas.

Despite these statistics, humandevelopment remains a high priorityfor Colombia, as indicated by the
government's strategy to improve people's education and skills formation (and through that, people's
productivityandincomes). The longtermvisionisthat 100percentof Colombianswillcomplete primary
andsecondaryeducationandhavethe rightskills to beinternationallycompetitive inthe labormarket and
earn a goodincome.

Government Strateg. President Uribe`s Government EducationPlan, the R & E h                      (Education
Reform), gives priorityto education, both based on the idea that it is a strategic input to the social and
economic development of the country and a fundamental humanright ina democratic society. Inline
with this approach, ithas adopted educationalpolicies aimedat guaranteeing access to andpermanencyin
the education system and improving the quality of education through better school governance and
administrative management.

The Government of Colombia is committedto sustained implementation of the educationreformagenda
andhas pushedfor the followingstrategic results for 2002-2006:

    ImedAms. Increase coverage by 1.4 million places inbasic (pre-school and prunary) and
    secondary education through efficiency gains from the reorganization of regional systems and the
    rationalization of resources

    schools to measure their learing achievement andpromote social accountability (b) systematic and
    regular measurement of teacher performance; and (c) performance-based incentive schemes to
    motivate teachers to teach more effectively andto attend trainingcourses for skills upgradmg.

the PISA process for the first time.95 The pilot was carried out inOctober 2005, and the full roundof
testingwas completed inMay2006, with results expected in2007

On the education supplyside, a process for evaluation of new teachers and principals to be hired in
2005/2006 was instituted. AU newteachers in2004-2006 who were hiredhadpassedthe entrance exams,
relative to a target of an 80 percent pass rate set by the LaRSDPL 111. The results of the exarm
demonstrated how importantthis tool is for increasing the qualityof educational services since over half
of the test-takers did not receive a passing gmde.96 Another 77 percent of municipalities are in the
process of testing andhmng. The end of operation result is that this criteria continues to be applied inat
least 80 percent of newhires.

Emected low-run impacts of actions sumorted bvthe DPL. The expansion of education coverage,
especially at the secondary education level, and improved quality of education should have a significant
impact in terms of poverty reduction and the construction of a democratic society with more equal
employment and income opportunities. Guaranteeing equity in human capital development through
education shouldcontribute to economic growth andimprovedincome distribution.

The expansion of quality and coverage is currently being funded through greater efficiency in the
allocation of teachug staff, greater utilization of the staff (increase inthe student-to-teacher ratio), and
contracting difficult services.  97  Further gains in efficiency will be more difficult to achieve, thus the
reliance onevenmore innovative service deliverymechanisms anddemandrngmore skilled teachers.

These gains have been difficult, as shown bythe ConstitutionalCourt's rejection of mandatory teacher
testing that was undertaken in2004 or the initialresistance to the reallocation of teachers. However, the
MENhasbeenadept atfindingalternative methodstoachievethesame objectives, andmanagingresults.
Nonetheless as the reforms for additional gains get more difficult, the potential for resistance among
affected educationalprofessionals mayincreases.

Other DroPress in the GO@s broader labor and social reform p r o m In terms of teacher
redeployment, the student to teacher ratio continues to increase. BetweenJuly2004 and February2005,
the ratio increased from 29.3 to 29.5, nearing the GoCs goal of 30.0 for 2006. These gains are smaller
than in the past since the easier gains, through staff reorganizations, were made earlier in the
administration.


95 OECD's Program for International Student Assessment (PIS4 measures how well 15-year-olds in school are
prepared to meet the challenges of todays knowledge societies. The assessment measures students' ability to use
their knowledge and skills to meet real-life challenges, as opposed to mastering facts or a specific school curriculum.
The first roundof PISAwas in2000 and includedmore than300,000 students in20 countries (inclulrLng non-OECD
members). It covered several content areas, but mostly focused on re+          literacy.  The second round, in2003,
which tested more than250,000 students from41countries, i n c l u d qMexico, Brad, andUruguay, focused more on
mathematics andproblem-solving ability. Since PISA goes b e p n d student achievement to provide a comprehensive
quantitative measure of the education system performance, it provides a useful perspective onthe effectiveness of a
country's overall education policy. Various countries have used previous PISA results to help transform or even
reform their education policy.

The MEN,with input fromICBF, is undertakmg the first effort inColombia to design and implement a
programfor universal preschooleducation (ages 3 to 6). Since July2004, the MENhas created quality
standards for pre-school education, prepared pedagogical materials based on these standards, trained
teachers inthe use of the materials, andl a u n c h a pilot programto evaluate pre-school competencies.

In addition to primary and secondary education, Colombia seeks to improve the productivity and
competitiveness of its economy through the reforms of technical secondary and tertiary education. The
national government is currently implementing reforms in secondary school to promote alternative
education paths to equip students with marketable competencies, by transforming the curriculum and

                                                        37

bringing the schools and the productive sectors closer. These reforms aim not only to improve the
countTy`s competitiveness, but also to increasethe levels of productive employment and therefore raising
the income of lower and middle class workers.              Aditionally, a student loan program (Acces) has
increased enrolment intechnical, technological andtertiaryeducationby65,000 students since 2002 (from
a base of 163,000).

The programto expand access to higher education with priorityto students from low income relies on
the establishment of CommunityCollege type institutions called "h de E~&aui& S q m b
                                                                                   Rqpmk
CERES".98 Since 2004, 66 new CERES have been launched (from a base of 22). Students attending
these institutions are beneficiaries of ICETEXstudent loans.




MainIssues &Lorntermvision. To increase efficiencyinsocial expenditures, itis necessaryto monitor
programexpenditures and outputs, carryout impact evaluation, andpubliclydebate these findings within
the Government and with civil society. Colombia has traditionallyhad a weak culture of evaluation,
leading to inefficient social spendmg and difficulties inidentifying anddesigning programimprovements,
which are p&ly            importantinthe current fiscallyconstrained environment. Breakingthis traditionis
difficult since those interested inshort-termfiscal balances may see such processes as unnecessary and
costly, and some program directors fear that impact evaluations are for the purpose of eliminating a
program, rather thanatoolfor improvingprogrameffectiveness.

Despite these challenges, early successes were achieved. In 1993, the National Planning Department
established a unit to focus on Mm. The primary role of the unit was to collect of hundreds of
monitoring indicators from across Government. In 2001, when the RAS was established, the GoC
agreed to invest in a serious impact evaluation program to determine the effectiveness of F d k en
Aadn,]&.ems enAadn, andEvph enAai&., thus launching the process to create, understand, anduse
impact evaluationas a public policytool.

Citizen's oversight does exist inColombia, but it can be strengthened. Inthe past, some Government
processes were open for public discussion, perhaps most importantly,the Constitutionof 1991 went
throughsuch aprocess, butthis was anexception, ratherthanarule. Littleinformationwas producedfor
public consumption, even though well organized sectorallyspecific civil society groups have existed for
years.99 Policymakers would benefit from public feedback in order to better provide the services that
their constituents mt, but such aprocess hadnotbeenwidelyestablished inthe Government.

                                                         38

The long term vision is to institute a culture of evaluation that will lead to a more efficient and results-
oriented government that responds to the needs of its citizens. This requires the creation of publicly
shared, easily accessible, and simply presented information about the public institutions and their
objectives, responsibilities, missions, goals, results, and impacts of their programs andpolicies. The most
costly programs would be regularly evaluated to improve the policymakq and resource allocation
process. It also requires the creation of an environment and tools where citizens and Government can
share information and exchange views. Finally, to determine if these efforts are successful, it requires the
development of indicators to determine if the public is participating inpolicymaking and the results of
suchinteraction.

Government Stratew. The GoCs strategy is to use periodic evaluations and participatory processes to
improve transparency and social control of social programs. In2002, the M&E agenda was taken to a
new level, partlydue to the Uribe Administration's strategy of results-based management: the National
Development Planlaidout quantitative targets, requiring a good systemto track progress toward meeting
these targets and an institutionalizationof a unit to systematize M&JL Making this a reality is a key
contribution to the country's M&E strategy. The Uribe Government also chose to place a lugh priority
on developing an impact evaluation agenda for better results management andgreater transparency. To
better use and monitor the quality of the information created, the Uribe Adminstration has given a
priorityto developing tools andprocessesto enhancetransparency andsocialovers&t.

contracted, to support the responsible institution and consultants by reviewing methodologies, results,
costs, andthe consequences of the findmgs inthe context of policymaking.
